Barnes,\n\nE-n.\n\nYeh, and R.E.\n\nEplee\n\nABSTRACT\nFor Earth-observing\nsatellite\ninstruments,\nit was standard\nto consider each instrument\nband to have a spectral\nresponse that is infinitely narrow, i.e., to have a response from a single wavelength.\nThe SeaWiFS\nbands, however,\nhave nominal spectral\nbandwidths\nof 20 and 40 nm. These bandwidths\naffect the SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\non\norbit. The effects are also linked to the manner in which the instrument\nwas calibrated\nand to the spectral\nshape\nof the radiance\nthat SeaWiFS\nviews. Currently,\nSeaWiFS\nis calibrated\nsuch that the digital counts from each\ninstrument\nband are linked to the Earth-exiting\nradiance\nat an individual\ncenter wavelength.\nBefore launch,\nSeaWiFS\nwill be recalibrated\nso that the digital counts from each band will be linked to the Earth-exiting\nradiance\nintegrated\nover the spectral\nresponse\nof that band.\nIn this technical\nmemorandum,\nthe effects of the\ninstrument\nout-of-band\n\ncalibration\nresponses,\n\nand the source spectral\nshape on SeaWiFS\nand the center wavelengths\nare discussed.\n\nPROLOGUE\nThe Sea-viewing\nWide Field-of-view\nSensor (SeaWiFS)\nmeasures\nthe Earth-exiting\nradiance at eight nominal center wavelengths\n(412, 443, 490, 510, 555, 670, 765, and\n865nm).\nIt was the practice\nin the retrieval\nof ocean\ncolor measurements\nto assume that the measurements\nfrom\nthe satellite\ninstrument\ncame from individual\nwavelengths\nand from individual\nwavelengths\nalone.\nThe SeaWiFS\nbands,\nhowever,\nalso have nominal\nspectral\nbandwidths\n(full-width\nat half-maximum\nresponse)\nof 20 and 40nm.\nThe incorporation\nof these broad spectral\nresponses\ninto\nthe data reduction\nalgorithms\nfor SeaWiFS\nwill lead to\nwater-leaving\nradiances\nthat are different from those based\non the nominal center wavelengths\nalone.\nFor SeaWiFS,\nas for many other satellite\nsensors, the\nperformance\nspecification\nfor the instrument\ndefines the\nin-band\nand the out-of-band\nresponses\nfor each measurement band.\nThe in-band\nresponse\nis defined\nas the response from the spectral\nregion between\nthe two 1% response points, that is, between the wavelengths\nat which\nthe response\nof the band is 1% of the maximum\nresponse.\nOut-of-band\nis defined as the response\nfrom wavelengths\noutside of those 1% points.\nFor SeaWiFS,\nthe out-of-band\nspecification\nallows up to 5% instrument\nresponse\nin the\nout-of-band\nwavelength\nregions for each band (Barnes\net\nal. 1994a). The actual out-of-band\nresponses\nfor the eight\nSeaWiFS\nbands (to a source with the spectral\nshape of a\n5,900 K blackbody)\nrange from 0.3-3.7% of the total band\nresponses\n(Barnes et al. 1994b). The out-of-band\nresponse\non orbit, however, will be a function of the spectral\nshape\nof the Earth-exiting\nradiance that SeaWiFS actually views.\nIn addition,\nthe laboratory\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS was\nperformed\nusing a spherical\nintegrating\nsource (SIS) that\nwas, itself, calibrated\nat the nominal SeaWiFS center wavelengths.\nThus, for SeaWiFS\nband 1, the counts from the\ninstrument\nin the laboratory\ncorresponded\nto a measured\nspectral\nradiance\nfrom the sphere\nat 412nm.\nA similar\npairing was made for the spectral\nradiances\nfrom the integrating\nsphere\nand the counts\nfrom the other SeaWiFS\n\nmeasurements,\n\nincluding\n\nthe\n\nbands.\nInformation\nfrom the instrument\nwho performed\nthe laboratory\ncalibration,\nthe spectral\nshape\nsphere approximated\ntype of calibration\n\nin-band\n\nand\n\nmanufacturer,\nindicated\nthat\n\nfor the radiance\nfrom the integrating\nthat for a 2,850 K blackbody.\nThis\nis consistent\nwith the calibration\nof\n\nother Earth-observing\nsatellite\ninstruments,\nsuch as the\nOcean Color and Temperature\nScanner\n(OCTS).\nAs a result, it was necessary\nto devise a procedure\nfor\ntransferring\nthe laboratory\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS\nto orbit. This was done by converting\nthe SeaWiFS\nlaboratory\nresponses\nfrom those for a source with a 2,850K\nspectral\nshape to those for a source with a 5,900 K spectral\nshape\n(Barnes et al. 1994b). The 5,900 K blackbody\nshape, which\napproximates\nthe spectral\nshape of the solar irradiance,\nwas the only spectral\nshape provided\nin the SeaWiFS\nperformance specification\n(Barnes et al. 1994a).\nThe output\nof the SeaWiFS\nbands (in digital counts)\nis linked to spectral radiances\nat the nominal center wavelengths.\nThis linkage leads to dependencies\nbetween\nthe\nbands\'\noutput\nand the spectral\nshapes\nof the sources\nviewed.\nThose dependencies\nare a direct consequence\nof\nthe manner\nin which SeaWiFS\nwas calibrated\nin the laboratory.\nThere is also a connection\nbetween\nthe response\nof the instrument\nat wavelengths\nwell away from the center wavelength\n(the out-of-band\nresponse)\nand the spectral\nshape of the sources that SeaWiFS\nviews.\nThis technical\nmemorandum\ncontains chapters\nthat address aspects of the dependence\nof the SeaWiFS bands--as\nthey have been calibrated\nby the manufacturer---on\nthe\nsource spectral\nshape.\nIn general,\nthe sources\nin these\nstudies are Planck function\n(blackbody)\ncurves of different temperatures.\nFor SeaWiFS,\nthe spectral\nshape of the\nlaboratory\nsource approximated\nthat for a 2,850 K blackbody. The shape of the solar output\nis that for a 5,900 K\nblackbody,\nand for the typical SeaWiFS\nradiances\nin the\ninstrument\nperformance\nspecification\n(Barnes et al. 1994a)\nthe spectral\nshape approximates\nthat for a 12,000 K blackbody (Barnes\net al. 1995a).\nFor the source dependence\nstudies in this document,\nthe reference spectral\nshapes are\n\nSeaWiFS alibration opics,\nC\nT\nPart1\nthose for biackbodies\nwith temperatures\nthat range from\n2,000 K to 30,000 K. Those studies are explained\nin Chapters 1 and 2.\nThe upwelling Earth radiance\nthat SeaWiFS\nwill view\non orbit,\nhowever,\ndoes not have a spectral\nshape that\nchanges smoothly\nwith wavelength\n(see Fig. 3 of Hooker et\nal. 1992). There are absorption\nlines in that spectrum,\nparticularly\nfrom water vapor and molecular\noxygen. Oxygen\nA-band absorption\ncan remove 10-15% of the in-band radiance viewed by SeaWiFS\nband 7 depending\non pathlengths\nwithin the atmosphere\n(Ding and Gordon\n1995 and Fraser\n1995]. For SeaWiFS\nband 8, water vapor can remove up\nto 1% of the upwelling\nradiation\nin its out-of-band\nregion\nfor very long pathlengths\nwithin the atmosphere\n(Gordon\n1995).\nIt is possible\nto adapt the studies\nrecounted\nin\nthis document\nto account\nfor these effects.\nThe absorption spectra\nfor molecular\noxygen and for water vapor are\navailable\nthrough\nthe LOWTI_N and MODTKAN atmospheric\nmodels.\nThe absorption\nspectra\nfor these gasses are the\nkernels of the modeling studies of Ding and Gordon (1995),\nFraser (1995), and Gordon (1995).\nThe LOWTRAN model for oxygen A-band\nabsorption\nfor\ntwo vertical\npasses through\nthe atmosphere\n(an airmass of\n2) is shown in Fig. 1. Other atmospheric\ntransmission\nfactors, such as absorption\nby water vapor and/or\nscattering\nby aerosols,\nare not shown.\nAs this figure shows, oxygen\nA-band\nabsorption\noccurs in the wavelength\nregion within\nwhich SeaWiFS\nband 7 responds\nto light best.\nA similar spectrum\nfor water vapor, with a water column amount of 3.338 gcm -2 and an airmass of 2, is shown\nin Fig. 2. This figure includes the relative spectral response\nfor SeaWiFS\nband 8. Atmospheric\nwater vapor, both as\na function\nof its column\namount\nand the pathlength\nfor\nsolar radiation\nreflected\nfrom the ocean surface, causes a\nsmall, but significant,\nreduction\nof upwelling\nradiation\nin\nthe spectral\nwings of band 8.\nSeaWiFS bands 7 and 8 form the basis for the SeaWiFS\natmospheric\ncorrection,\na correction\nthat is critical to the\ncalculation\nof SeaWiFS\nwater-leaving\nradiances.\nAs shown\nin Figs. 1 and 2, oxygen and water vapor do not absorb at\nindividual\nwavelengths,\nbut over wavelength\nregions that\nextend\nfrom a few to more than 100nm.\nThus, based on\nthe modelling\nresults of Gordon (1995), it was decided by\nthe Calibration\nSubgroup\nof the SeaWiFS\nScience Working\nGroup that SeaWiFS\nshould have a laboratory\ncalibration\nwhich includes\nthe response of each instrument\nband to\nupwelling Earth radiance\nover its full spectral\nrange.\nThis response is known as the band-weighted\nspectral\nradiance\n(BSR). It is the spectral\nradiance\nof the source\nintegrated\nover the spectral\nresponse\nof the band.\nTo be\nuseful for Earth observations,\neach BSR must be associated\nwith a band-weighted\ncenter wavelength\n(BCW). The calibration\nof SeaWiFS\nfor BSRs has not been performed\nas\nyet. This calibration\nawaits the final thermal-vacuum\ntesting of SeaStar,\nthe spacecraft\nthat will carry SeaWiFS;\nthe\ncalibration\nis currently\nscheduled\nfor the fourth quarter of\n\n2\n\n1996.\n\nIt will be consistent\n\nwith\n\nthe\n\ncalibration\n\nof other\n\nEarth-observing\nsatellite\ninstruments,\nsuch as the Moderate Resolution\nImaging\nSpectroradiometer\n(MODIS).\nBefore the postthermal-vacuum\ncalibration\ncan occur,\nthree things must be known: the spectral\nshape of the radiance from the laboratory\nsource; the spectral\nresponse\nof\nthe SeaWiFS\nbands; and the spectral\nshape of the bands\nin the reference\nradiometer.\nThe spectral\nresponse\nof SeaWiFS is known (Barnes\n1994). The spectral\nshape of the\nradiance\nfrom the GSFC sphere, which will be used as the\nsource in the calibration,\nis described\nin Chapter\n1. And,\nthe spectral\nshape of the SeaWiFS\nTransfer\nRadiometer\n(SXR), which will be used as the reference\nradiometer\nin\nthe calibration,\nis discussed\nin Chapter\n3.\nIn addition to a calibration\nin terms of BSRs, the (future) postthermal-vacuum\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS can also\nbe interpreted\nin the same manner\nas the original\nlaboratory calibration\nof SeaWiFS\nby the manufacturer,\nthat is,\nin terms of spectral\nradiances\nat the eight nominal\nSeaWiFS center wavelengths.\nSo, before launch, SeaWiFS will\nbe calibrated\nin two ways: in terms of the BSRs from each\nband, and in terms of the spectral\nradiances\nat tile nominal\ncenter wavelengths\nfor each band. For the BSRs, a corresponding\ncenter wavelength\nmust be determined\nfor each\nband.\nThese center wavelengths\nare described\nin Chapter 4.\nFinally, the effects of absorption\nby atmospheric\nwater\nvapor and oxygen on SeaWiFS\nbands 7 and 8 can be calculated for measurements\nthat use the original SeaWiFS\ncalibration.\nFor this type of calibration,\nin which the counts\nfrom the bands are linked to spectral radiances\nat the nominal center wavelengths\nfor the bands, the absorption\ncorrections require a nominal\ntop-of-the-atmosphere\nspectral\nradiance\ncurve, plus models for the spectral\nshapes and\nintensities\nof the molecular\nabsorption\nlines.\nFor water\nvapor, which absorbs in the wings of the band responses,\nLOWTRANabsorption\nvalues, with their relatively\nlow wavelength resolution,\nare sufficient.\nFor oxygen, which absorbs\nin the center of the response\nfor band 7, MODTRANspectra,\ninterpolated\nto 0.1 nm intervals,\nare required.\nThese data\nsets have been compiled,\nand the calculated\nresults will be\npresented\nin a subsequent\ntechnical\nmemorandum\nin this\nseries. A short synopsis of each chapter\nin this volume is\ngiven below.\n1.\n\nCalculation\nof an Equivalent\nTemperature\nfor the GSFC\nThe\n\nSeaWiFS\n\nradiometer\n\nshares\n\nBlackbody\nSphere\na number\n\nof funda-\n\nmental characteristics\nwith other filter-based\nspectrometers. Among these characteristics\nis a dependence\nof the\nthroughput\nof the interference\nfilters on the spectral\nshape\nof the measured\nradiance.\nThis dependence\nvaries from\nband to band within the instrument\nand is based on the\nspectral\nresponse\nof each individual\nband. The radiance curves are normalized\nat\nthe nominal\nSeaWiFS\ncenter wavelengths,\nthat is, at the\nwavelengths\nused in the laboratory\ncalibration\nof the instrument.\nThe model results show that, as far as the eight\nSeaWiFS\nbands are concerned,\nthe radiance curves for the\nGoddard\nSpace Flight Center\n(GSFC) SIS are equivalent\nto that for a 2,850K\nblackbody.\nThe model results also\nshow that the output\nfrom the SeaWiFS\nbands will have\ndifferences\nof up to 5% on orbit, when compared\nwith the\nradiometric\ncalibration\nin the laboratory.\n2.\n\nEffects of Source Spectral\nShape\nSea WiFS Radiance\nMeasurements\n\non\n\nTwo aspects of filter photometry\non the radiances\nmeasured by SeaWiFS are examined:\nthe dependence\nof the total band response,\nand the out-of-band\nresponse\non source\nspectral\nshape. These effects are significant\ncontributors\nto\nthe SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\non orbit. The corrections\nfor\nout-of-band\nresponse\nrange from about 0.5% to more than\n5%, depending\non the SeaWiFS\nband. The corrections\nfor\ntotal band response\nrange from near zero to about\n5%.\nThese corrections\nwill be used to convert\nthe laboratory\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS\n(in terms of total band response)\nto orbit (in terms of in-band response).\nThe residual uncertainty in these corrections\nis estimated\nto be small, around\n0.2%. With other significantly\nlarger error sources in the\nSeaWiFS\nmeasurements,\nthe contribution\nof these residual\nuncertainties\nto the propagation\nof error for SeaWiFS\nradiance measurements\nat the top of the atmosphere\nis also\nexpected\nto be small. These corrections\nare prelaunch\nestimates\nand should be reexamined\nusing flight data.\nIn\naddition,\nthese corrections\nhave been tailored to SeaWiFS\nocean measurements.\nThe contributions\nto land measurements will be different.\n3.\n\nA Comparison\nof the Spectral\nResponses\nof SeaWiFS\nand the\nSea WiFS Transfer Radiometer\nBoth\n\nfinite\n\nSeaWiFS\n\nbandwidths.\n\nand the SXR are filter radiometers\nThe\n\nuse of these\n\ninstruments\n\na knowledge\nof the effects that finite bandwidths\nThese effects also depend\non the source\'s\nspectral\n\nwith\nrequires\ncause.\nshape\n\nTopics,\n\nPart\n\n1\n\nwhich the instruments\nmeasure.\nFor the radiometric\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe source is the GSFC SIS. The\nBSRs and the effective center wavelengths\n(ECWs) for SeaWiFS and the SXR, when measuring\nthe GSFC SIS, are\npresented\nhere. They will be needed to convert the SXR\nmeasured\nspectral\nradiances\nto the SeaWiFS wavelengths.\n4.\n\nSeaWiFS\n\nCenter\n\nWavelengths\n\nWith its 20 and 40 nm bandwidths,\nSeaWiFS\ndoes not\nmake measurements\nat individual\nwavelengths.\nRather,\nthe output from each SeaWiFS band can be considered\nas\nan integral\nover a finite wavelength\nregion of the source\nit measures.\nTo make SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\nuseful, it is\nimportant\nto relate the measured\nradiance from the instrument to a reference wavelength.\nThat wavelength\nconnects\neach SeaWiFS\nmeasurement\nto the spectral\nradiance from\nthe source at that wavelength.\nFor the initial calibration\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe reference\ncenter wavelengths\nwere those\nfrom the instrument\'s\nperformance\nspecifications.\nA set\nof BCWs for on-orbit\nuse are presented\nhere. The use of\nthese new wavelengths\nwill require\na revision\nof the onorbit radiometric\ncalibration\nconstants\nfor the instrument.\n5.\n\nThe\nMeasurements\n\nSeaWiFS\nof the\n\nsolar\n\nSolar Diffuser\nflux\n\nwith\n\nan onboard\n\nso-\n\nlar diffuser will provide an important\ncheck of changes in\nthe radiometric\nsensitivity\nof SeaWiFS\nover the duration\nof the instrument\'s\non-orbit\nmeasurements\nof ocean color.\nAlthough\ndiffuser measurements\ndo not give an absolute\ndetermination\nof radiometric\nchanges,\nthey will identify\nany rapid changes that may occur in the instrument\noutput. The diffuser was designed to produce\nradiance\nlevels\nthat approximate\nthose for other SeaWiFS measurements.\nThis design includes an attenuator\nto reduce the solar radiance levels on the diffuser, and hence, the effects of photolyzed organic compounds.\nThe BRDF, which shows the\noutput of the diffuser as a function of pitch and yaw angle,\nhas been determined.\nThis allows the removal of angular dependencies\nfrom the long-term\nradiometric\nsensitivity data.\nIn addition,\noutdoor\nmeasurements\nhave given\nthe predicted\noutput from SeaWiFS\nfor initial on-orbit\nsolar diffuser measurements.\n\nR.A.Barnes,\nE-n.Yeh,andR.E.Eplee\n\nChapter\nCalculation\nTemperature\n\nof an\n\nEquivalent\nfor the GSFC\n\nROBERT\nGeneral\n\n1\nBlackbody\nSphere\n\nA. BARNES\n\nSciences Corporation\nLaurel, Maryland\nABSTRACT\n\nThe SeaWiFS\nradiometer\nshares\nseveral fundamental\ncharacteristics\nwith other filter-based\nspectrometers.\nAmong these characteristics\nis a dependence\nof the throughput\nof the interference\nfilters on the spectral\nshape\nof the measured\nradiance.\nThis dependence\nvaries from band to band within the instrument\nand is based on\nthe spectral\nresponse\nof each individual\nband.\nHere, the measured\nspectral\nresponse\ncurves for the SeaWiFS\nbands and a set of radiance\ncurves to provide a model for this dependence\nare used. The radiance\ncurves are\nnormalized\nat the nominal\nSeaWiFS\ncenter wavelengths,\nthat is, at the wavelengths\nused in the laboratory\ncalibration\nof the instrument.\nThe model results show that, as far as the eight SeaWiFS\nbands are concerned,\nthe radiance\ncurves for the GSFC SIS are equivalent\nto that for a 2,850K\nblackbody.\nThe model results also\nshow that the output\nfrom the SeaWiFS\nbands will have differences of up to 5% on orbit, when compared\nwith\nthe radiometric\ncalibration\nin the laboratory.\n\n1.1\n\nINTRODUCTION\nThe construction\n\nand calibration\n\nof SeaWiFS\n\nwas com-\n\npleted in November\n1993. The instrument\nwas designed by\nthe Santa Barbara\nResearch\nCenter (SBRC) to meet a set\nof performance\nspecifications,\nincluding\nspecifications\nfor\nmaximum\nocean radiances\nand maximum\ncloud radiances\n(Barnes\net al. 1994a).\nTo determine\nthe instrument\noutput for these radiance levels, SBRC used a 100 cm SIS with\na calibration\ntraceable\nto the National\nInstitute\nof Standards and Technology\n(NIST). The SBRC sphere used sets\nof 200W,\n45 W, and 5 W tungsten\nlamps.\nThe spectral\nshape for the sphere output was assumed\nto be equivalent\nto a 2,850 K blackbody\n(Barnes et al. 1994b).\nThe output\nof the GSFC 106cm SIS was characterized and calibrated\nby NIST in April 1995. The results\nof this characterization\nwill be published\nas part of a future SeaWiFS\ntechnical\nmemorandum.\nThe absolute\ncalibration\nof the sphere will change with time as the lamps\nand the reflective\ninner surface of the sphere\nage. The\nuse of the sphere will require frequent\nrecalibration\nusing\na transfer\nradiometer\nbefore it is viewed by the instruments under test.\nHowever,\nother characteristics\nof the\nsphere--the\nareal uniformity\nof the sphere\'s\nradiant\noutput across its output\naperture\nand the spectral\nshape of\nits output--need\nto be checked much less frequently.\nAs\npart of the characterization\nof the sphere at NIST, its radiance was measured\nrelative to a NIST-standard\ngold-point\n\nblackbody,\nusing equipment\nand techniques\ndescribed\nin\nWalker et al. (1987). For these measurements,\nthe spectral\nresponse\nof the GSFC sphere has a maximum\nradiance\nat\n890 nm (Fig. 3a). Using the Wein displacement\nlaw (Wyatt 1978) this wavelength\ncorresponds\nto a Planck function\n(blackbody)\ntemperature\nof 3,256 K.\nThe GSFC and SBRC integrating\nspheres were not designed to duplicate\nideal flat-black\nradiators\nthat emit at\nspecific temperatures.\nThis non-ideal\nspectral\noutput\nfor\nthe GSFC sphere is shown in Fig. 3. The output\nfrom the\nsphere at 890 nm is less than 1 part in 10,000 of that from\na 3,256K blackbody,\nand normalization\nof the blackbody\ncurve was required\nto create the figure.\nThis gives the\nsphere an extremely\nsmall emissivity\n(e), less than 0.0001.\nIn addition,\nthe GSFC sphere cannot be treated\nas a greybody, since its emissivity\nchanges\nwith wavelength.\nThis\nalso can be seen in Fig. 3, where the sphere output\ndoes\nnot follow the shape of the blackbody\nfor wavelengths\naway\nfrom 890 nm.\nThe data from the characterization\nof the GSFC sphere\nare sufficient to calculate\nemissivity\nas a function of wavelength.\nThese data can also be acquired\nfor the SBRC\nsphere.\nSuch characterizations\nof the integrating\nspheres,\nhowever, are not necessary.\nFor the SeaWiFS\nProject,\ninterest is limited to the manner\nin which the sphere output\neffects the eight SeaWiFS\nbands.\nEach SeaWiFS\nband\nhas its own spectral\nresponse\n(Barnes\net al. 1994b), and\nthat spectral\nresponse\ninteracts\nwith the spectral\nshape of\n\n5\n\nSeaWiFSalibration\nC\n\nRodionce\nSymbols\nCurve\n\n5o\n\nfrom\n-\n\nthe\n3256K\nMeosured\n\nGSFC\n\nSphere\n\nBlockbody\nRadiances\n\nTopics,\n\n(I6\n\nPart\n\n1\n\nIomps)\n\nNormalized\n\not\n\n890\n\nnm\n\ni 40\nE\n"T 3o\n\n?\nE\n\xe2\x80\xa2 2O\nE\n._ 10o\n\nWavelength\n\nFig.\nThe\n\n(nrn)\n\n3. Such interactions,\nincluding\nthose for the output\nfrom the GSFC sphere, are presented\nhere. Those responses\nwere described\npreviously\n(Barnes et al. 1994b) for three different types of sources: a\n2,850K\nblackbody,\na 5,900K\nblackbody,\nand a spectrally\nfiat source.\nThe spectrally\nfiat responses\nwere calculated\n(on a nanometer\nby nanometer\nbasis) using the spectral responses of the filters, lenses, and dichroics in each band\'s\noptical train.\nFor each of these components,\nthe response\n\nwith the ratio of electrons\nout to photons\nin\nmilliamperes\nper milliwatt.\nThus,\nat each\ninterval\nfrom 380-1,150nm,\nthe output\nfrom\ntrain for each band has units of mA mW-1\n\nHere, as in Barnes et al. (1994b), the piece-part\nderived\nresponse ratios have been multiplied\nat each nanometer\nby\n1 mW cm -2 sr -1 p m-t to give the band\'s response-to-unit\nradiance.\nThe output\nfor SeaWiFS\nband 6 is shown in\nFig. 4a. The output for this and the other SeaWiFS\nbands\nis the current from the photodiode.\nAs shown in Fig. 4a for\nband 6 and a spectrally\nfiat source, the current\nfrom the\nphotodiode\nis the sum of the current at each nanometer\ninterval; this integrated\ncurrent is a few nanoamperes.\nThis\ncurrent\ncan be used to determine\nthe relative response\nof\nthe instrument\nto sources with different\nspectral\nshapes;\nhowever, it cannot determine\nthe output of the instrument\non an absolute\nbasis. For SeaWiFS\nocean measurements, the reflected\nlight from the sea surface is a small\nfraction\nof the sky radiance.\nThis radiance\nis dominated\nby scattering\nfrom air molecules.\nMolecular,\nor Rayleigh,\nscattering\nof unpolarized\nlight\nby anisotropic\nair\nwith a dependence\nmated by the form\n\nmolecules\nhas\non wavelength\nA-4 (Penndorf\n\na =\n\na scattering\n(A) which\n1957).\n\ncoefficient\nis approxi-\n\n8_3(n_ - 1)2D\n3A4\nNs2\n,\n\n(4)\n\nwhere a is the Rayleigh\nscattering\ncross section per molecule (cm2); ns is the refractive\nindex of air (dimensionless); D is the depolarization\nfactor which expresses the influence of the molecular\nanisotropy\nof air (dimensionless);\nand Ns is the number\ndensity of the scattering\nmolecules\n(cIn-3).\n\nThere is an additional\nwavelength\ndependence\nin the\nRayleigh\nscattering\ncoefficient,\nsince the coefficient\nalso\nincludes\nthe refractive\nindex of air, which has its own dependence\non wavelength.\nSuch a refinement\nis unnecessary\nhere, since the additional\nwavelength\ndependence\nis small,\nand since the blue sky radiance is itself an approximation\nto the sky radiance.\nThe actual upwelling Earth radiance\nseen by SeaWiFS\nalso includes contributions\nfrom aerosol\nscattering,\nabsorption\nand emission\nfrom gasses, and reflections from the surface of the Earth.\nThe\n\nmodel\n\ncalculations\n\npresented\n\nspecial\nsource spectral\nshapes:\nthe spectral\nradiance\nfrom the\n\n10\n\nhere\n\ncontain\n\nthree\n\nthe measured\nshape for\nGSFC integrating\nsphere;\n\nTopics,\n\nPart\n\n1\n\nthe spectral\nshape of a 5,900 K blackbody,\nwhich approximates\nthe spectral\nshape of the solar flux; and a spectral shape with a wavelength\ndependence\nof A-4, which\napproximates\nthe upwelling\nradiance\nfrom a molecular\n(or\nRayleigh) atmosphere.\nThe model calculations\nalso include\nPlanck functions\nwith temperatures\nfrom 2,000-38,000\nK.\nThese Planck functions\nencompass\nthe three special spectral shapes.\n\n1.3\n\nMODEL\n\nRESULTS\n\nThe integrated\noutput\nfor the eight SeaWiFS\nare listed in Table 2. The results are also shown\n(bands 1 and 2), Fig. 7 (bands 3 and 4), Fig. 8\nand 6), and Fig. 9 (bands 7 and 8). For Figs. 6-9,\nimum values for the ordinates\nhave not been set\n\nbands\nin Fig. 6\n(bands 5\nthe minto zero.\n\nThis was done to increase resolution\nfor the relatively\nsmall\nchanges in the output from the bands.\nTo give some consistency to the vertical scales, however, the maximum\nvalues\nfor the ordinates\nhave been set to about\n1.2-1.25\ntimes\nthe minimum\nvalues. The changes\nin the integrated\nband\noutput are shown in Table 3, normalized\nto the output\nfor\na 5,900 K blackbody.\nFor the prelaunch\nradiometric\ncalibration of SeaWiFS\n(Barnes et al. 1994b), the coefficients\nfor the calibration\nequations\nwere calculated,\nbased on a\n5,900 K blackbody.\nThe equivalent\nblackbody\ntemperatures\nfor the GSFC\nsphere and the Rayleigh\natmosphere\nare also shown\nin\nFigs. 6-9. These temperatures\nwere determined\nby interpolation,\nusing the band output\nfor the blackbody\nradiances. The equivalent\nGSFC sphere temperatures\nare near\n3,000K and the equivalent\nRayleigh\natmosphere\ntemperatures range from 25,000-35,000\nK. This consistency\nwas\nthe basis for the selection\nof the equivalent\ntemperature\nwhen two possible solutions\nwere possible.\nThe NIST measurements\ninclude spectral\nresponses\nfor\nfour configurations\nof the sphere: 1 lamp, 4 lamps, 8 lamps,\nand 16 lamps illuminated.\nThe radiances\nfrom these output cover a range of radiance\nlevels that is sufficient\nfor\nthe calibration\nof the SeaWiFS\nradiometer.\nThe radiances\nfrom the NIST measurements,\ninterpolated\nto the nominal\nSeaWiFS center wavelengths,\nare listed in Table 4.\nThe SeaWiFS\nintegrated\nband output\nfor the GSFC\nsphere radiances\n(Table 2) contain\nthe average values for\nthe four lamp settings.\nTable 5 gives the Sea_,ViFS integrated\nband output\ntbr each of the four NIST spectral\ncurves.\nThe mean values in Table 5 have small standard\ndeviations.\nAs a result, the results for the mean integrated\nband output\nare interchangeable\nwith those for the individual lamp settings.\nIt should be stressed\nthat reality\nfor this discussion\nis based on the spectral\nresponse\ncurves for the SeaWiFS\nbands.\nSeaWiFS\nradiance\nmeasurements\nfrom the GSFC\nsphere are not presented\nhere, and, in fact, there are no\nSeaWiFS\nradiance\nmeasurements\nin this report.\nThe resuits are based on model radiances\nonly.\n\nR.A.Barnes,\nE-n.Yeh,andR.E.E_Jlee\n\nTable2. The SeaWiFS\nband output, when viewing the GSFC sphere,\nare essentially\nthe same as when\nviewing\na 2,850K\nblackbody.\nOn the average,\nthe integrated\nband output\nfor the GSFC sphere is 0.07% lower\nthan for a 2,850 K blackbody,\nwith a 0.15% standard\ndeviation. For individual\nbands, the differences\nbetween these\ntwo sources are 0.25% percent or less. Thus, for SeaWiFS,\nthe GSFC sphere and a 2,850 K blackbody\nhave equivalent\nspectral\nshapes.\nFor the radiance\nsource used in SBRC\'s calibration\nof\nSeaWiFS,\nthe spectral\nshape of the source was assumed\nto be 2,850 K, and no measurements\nof the source spectral\nshape were provided\n(Barnes et al. 1994b). Such a minimal\ndependence\nof the SeaWiFS output\non spectral\nshape (between 2,800 and 3,000K)\nshows that inaccuracies\nin the\nmanufacturer\'s\nassumption\ncontribute\nnegligible\nerror to\nthe instrument\'s\nradiometric\ncalibration.\n1.4\n\n[pA]\nBand\n\nREMARKS\n\nFilter photometers\nin general, and SeaWiFS\nin particular, have output\nthat vary with the spectral\nshape of the\nsource that they measure.\nFor SeaWiFS,\nthe transfer\nof\nthe laboratory\ncalibration\nto orbit requires\nsource shape\ncorrections\nfor the eight bands that vary from near zero\n(for band 4) to more than 5% (for band 8). From Table 3,\nit is clear that the correction\nfrom a 2,850 K to a 5,900 K\nsource (Barnes et al. 1994b) does not adequately\naccount\nfor the upwelling radiance\nthat SeaWiFS\nwill view on orbit.\nThis said, there remain two additional\nfactors that\nmust be considered.\nFirst, Rayleigh\nscattering\ndoes not completely\naccount\nfor the spectral\nshape of the Earth-exiting\nradiances\nthat\n\nSeaWiFS\nwill view on orbit.\nThere\nare absorption\nand\nemission\nlines from atmospheric\ngasses within the wavelength region between 380 and 1,150 nm. The most important of these features are the oxygen absorption\nlines near\n762nm (McClain\net al. 1994, Fraser 1995, and Ding and\nGordon\n1995) that lie within the bandpass\nfor SeaWiFS\nband 7. These absorption\nlines create an oxygen\nnotch\nwithin band 7, and they must be treated\nexplicitly\nin the\nSeaWiFS data reduction\nalgorithms.\nIn addition, scattered\nlight from small atmospheric\nparticles,\ni.e., Mie scattering,\ncreates\nan additional\nsource\nof Earth-exiting\nradiance.\nSince Rayleigh\nscattering\ndecreases as a function\nof wavelength\nto the fourth\npower,\nMie scattering\nbecomes\nmore important\nfor wavelengths\nin the red and near-infrared.\nAs the number of small particles in the atmosphere\nincreases,\nthe sky loses its blue\ncolor and turns white. Thus, the calculations\npresented\nin\nthis chapter cover the color range of the Earth-exiting\nradiances viewed by SeaWiFS\nduring ocean measurements.\nIn addition,\nthese results show the magnitude\nof the effects\nof source spectral shape on the integrated\noutput\nfrom the\nSeaWiFS\nbands. This leads to the second factor that must\nbe considered.\nFor SeaWiFS\nband 6 (Fig. 4), the region of greatest\nresponse,\ncalled the in-band\nresponse,\noccurs\nfrom 647678nm\n(Barnes\net al. 1994a).\nIt is the in-band\nresponse\nthat is required from the on-orbit\nSeaWiFS measurements,\nbut it is the total band response\nthat is measured\nin the\nlaboratory.\nThe SeaWiFS\nin-band\nresponses\nhave source\nshape dependencies,\nas do the SeaWiFS\nout-of-band\nresponses.\nTogether\nthey create the source\nshape dependency for the total band response.\nBoth must be accounted\nfor in order to convert the laboratory\ncalibration\nof the instrument\nto the desired on-orbit\nvalues (Barnes\nand Yeh\n1996).\n\n17\n\nSeaWiFS alibration\nC\nTopics,\nPart1\n\nChapter\nEffects\nof\nSeaWiFS\n\n2\n\nSource\nSpectral\nShape\nRadiance\nMeasurements\n\non\n\nROBERT A. BARNES\nEUENG-NAN YEH\nGeneral\n\nSciences\n\nCorporation,\n\nLaurel,\n\nMaryland\n\nABSTRACT\nTwo aspects\nof filter photometry\non the radiances\nmeasured\nby SeaWiFS\nare examined:\nthe dependence\nof\nthe total band response,\nand the out-of-band\nresponse\non source spectral\nshape.\nThese effects are significant\ncontributors\nto the SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\non orbit. The corrections\nfor out-of-band\nresponse range from about\n0.5% to more than 5%, depending\non the SeaWiFS\nband. The corrections\nfor total band response\nrange from\nnear zero to about 5%. These corrections\nwill be used to convert the laboratory\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS\n(in terms\nof total band response)\nto orbit (in terms of in-band response).\nThe residual uncertainty\nin these corrections\nestimated\nto be small, around 0.2%. With other significantly\nlarger error sources in the SeaWiFS\nmeasurements,\nthe contribution\nof these residual uncertainties\nto the propagation\nof error for SeaWiFS\nradiance\nmeasurements\nat the top of the atmosphere\nis also expected\nto be small. These corrections\nare prelaunch\nbe reexamined\nusing flight data. In addition,\nthese corrections\nwere tailored to SeaWiFS\nThe contributions\nto land measurements\nwill be different.\n\n2.1\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nBarnes et al. (1994b)\nsponses\nof the SeaWiFS\n\ncalculated\nthe change in the rebands from those for a 2,850K\n\nblackbody\n(the spectral\nshape of the radiance\nsource for\nthe SeaWiFS\nlaboratory\ncalibration)\nto those for a 5,900 K\nblackbody\n(the spectral\nshape of the sun). This conversion\nwas used in the prelaunch\nradiometric\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS\n(Barnes\net al. 1994b).\nAn alternative\nconversion\nto a spectral\nshape of a 12,000K\nblackbody\nis provided\nin this chapter.\nThis spectral\nshape is consistent\nwith the\nSeaWiFS typical radiances\nin the instrument\nspecifications\n(Barnes\net al. 1994a) and gives a better representation\nof\nthe radiances\nto be found during on-orbit\nocean measurements by SeaWiFS.\nBarnes et al. (1995a) presented\nan out-of-band\ncorrection scheme for SeaWiFS\nthat used a (:ombination\nof the\nwavelength-dependent\nresponse\nof the instrument\nwith the\nwavelength-dependent\nshape of the radiance\nmeasured\nby\nthe instrument.\nThe relative\nspectral\nresponses\nfor the\neight SeaWiFS bands were tabulated\nat 1 nm intervals from\n380-1,150nm\n(Barnes et al. 1994b and Barnes 1994). As\npart of the calculations\nin Barnes et al. (1995a), the spectral radiances\nfrom the eight SeaWiFS bands were interpolated to 1 nm intervals\nand were used as the basis for the\nspectral\nshape\nThe radiances\n\n18\n\nof the radiance\nand spectral\n\nmeasured\nresponses\n\nby the instrument.\nof the bands were\n\nis\n\nestimates\nand should\nocean measurements.\n\nintegrated\nfrom 380-1,150nm\n(for the total band output\nfor each band) and from the lower extended\nband edge to\nthe upper extended\nband edge (for the in-band\nresponse).\nWith 771 points in the integration\nfor each band, 8 bands\nper pixel, 1,285 pixels per scan line, and 6 scan lines per\nsecond in the on-orbit measurements,\nthe out-of-band\ncorrection of Barnes et al. (1995a) provides a cumbersome\nand\ntime-consuming\nalgorithm\nfor processing\nSeaWiFS\ndata.\nIn this chapter,\nthe analysis method\nused in Barnes et al.\n(1995a)\nis used to create simplified\nout-of-band\ncalculations which run significantly\nfaster in the SeaWiFS\ndata\nreduction.\n\n2.2\n\nBAND\n\nSPECIFICATIONS\n\nThe SeaWiFS specifications\n(Barnes et al. 1994a) define\nthe in-band response\nregion for each SeaWiFS\nband. It is\nthe spectral\nregion between the lower extended\nband edge\n(lower 1_ response\npoint) and the upper extended\nband\nedge (upper 1_ response\npoint).\nThe band edges for the\neight SeaWiFS\nbands, taken from Barnes et al. (1994b),\nare given in Table 6. As a result, an accurate and well-defined\nout-of-band\ncorrection\nfor this band\nis crucial for SeaWiFS\ndata reduction.\nBand 8 also shows\nthe greatest\nchange in the size of its out-of-band\nwith\nsource spectral\nshape. Those ntypical\nare shown\nin Fig. 19,\n\n33\n\nSeaWiFS alibration\nC\nTopics,\nPart1\nTable11. Theinitial out-of-band correction factors are listed here. Although\nnot a perfect fit, the Ltypical\nradiances\nare approximated\nby a 12,000K\nblackbody.\nAs shown in Tables 7-10, the\nresponses\nof the SeaWiFS\nbands are nearly\nidentical\nfor\nPlanck function curves of 10,000 K; 12,000 K; and 14,000 K.\nFor the purposes\nhere, it is assumed\nthat the typical Sea.\nWiFS radiances\nfor ocean measurements\nhave the spectral\nshape\n\n2.5\n\nof a 12,000 K blackbody.\n\nOUT-OF-BAND\n\n2.5.1\n\nBands\n\nTables\n\n7 and\n\nCORRECTION\n\n1-4\n8 show the\n\nratios\n\nof the in-band\n\nto the\n\ntotal-band\nresponses\n(for bands 1-4) to be about 0.9900.995 for blackbody\nspectral curves between 12,000 K and\n38,000 K. For each of these bands, the change in the outof-band correction factor is negligible--between\n12,000K\nand 38,000 K; thus, for bands 1-4 the 12,000K correction\nfactor is used in the simplified correction scheme proposed\nhere (Table 11). As shown for band 1 (412 nm), the outof-band correction is applied using the formula:\nLo(412)\n\n= kb(412)Li(412),\n\n(5)\n\nwhere\nL,(412)\nis the input\nradiance\nto the correction\nscheme\nfor band 1 (412nm);\nkb(412 ) is the out-of-band\ncorrection\nfactor for band 1 (412 nm); and Lo(412) is the\noutput\nradiance\nfrom the correction\nscheme\nfor band 1\n(412 nm). It is the k b correction\nfactor for each SeaWiFS\nband that this simplified\ncalculation\nscheme provides.\nFor\nbands 1-4, k b is a constant\nvalue, listed in Table 11.\nFor the SeaWiFS\nProject,\nthere is a naming convention\nfor the radiances\nfrom the eight bands. In this convention,\nthe bands are not described\nin terms of their number\n(1\nthrough\n8) but in terms of their nominal center wavelength\n(412, 443, 490, 510, 555, 670, 765, and 865nm).\nThis\nconvention\nis also applied in the equations\nused here.\n\n34\n\nalso taken from Table 7, 12,000 K\nalso taken from Table 7, 12,000K\nalso taken from Table 8, 12,000 K\nalso taken from Table 8, 12,000K\ncalculated\nusing (9)\ncalculated\nusing (12)\nalso taken from Table 10, 12,000K\nabsorption\n(Section 2.5.2, below)\ncalculated\nusing (14)\n\n2.5.2\n\nBand\n\nand corrected\n\n7\n\nFor SeaWiFS\nband 7 (the 765nm band),\nthere is an\natmospheric\noxygen absorption\nfeature in the center of the\npass band response\n(McClain\net al. 1994, Fraser 1995, and\nDing and Gordon 1995). This oxygen A-band\nabsorption\ndoes not effect the out-of-band\nresponse\nfor the 765 nm\nband.\nUsing the same technique,\nMcClain\net al. (1994)\nand Fraser (1995) both estimated\nthe reduction\nin the output of band 7 from this absorption\nfeature to be 12% for\nan air mass of 2, and 13% for an air mass of 3. Ding and\nGordon (1995) used a separate\ncalculation\nto estimate\nthe\nreduction\nin Rayleigh\nscattered\nlight at the top of the atmosphere from oxygen absorption\nto be 7% for an air mass\nof 2, and 11% for an air mass of 5. Because\nthe out-ofband contribution\nto the output\nof band 7 is close to zero\n(about 1.5%), the error in the calculated\neffect of oxygen\nabsorption\non the out-of-band\ncorrection\nis also small. For\nthe calculations\nhere, oxygen absorption\nis assumed to decrease the in-band response\nof SeaWiFS\nband 7 by 12%.\nThe modification\nof band 7\'s correction\nfactor, kb(765 ),\nis shown in Table 12. In this table, the total band, the inband, and the out-of-band\nresponses\n(for a 12,000 K blackbody) were taken from Table 10. The effect of oxygen absorption was calculated\nby removing\n12% from the in-band\nresponse\n(338.568 pA) and by removing\nthe same number\nof picoamperes\nfrom the total band response.\nThe out-ofband response\nremains constant.\nThe revised out-of-band\ncorrection\nfactor is calculated\nas the ratio of the revised\nin-band response\nto the revised total response.\nThat ratio\nis 0.9844, and this value is given in Table 11 for the band\n7 correction,\nkb(765).\nThus, the out-of-band\ncorrection\nfor\nband 7 is:\nLo(765)\n\n-- kb(765)Li(765),\n\n(6)\n\nwhere Lo(765) is the output\nfrom this correction\nscheme\nfor band 7. For band 1,\nthe adjustment\nin Table 17 differs from that in Barnes et\nal. (1994b)\n\nby\n\n1%.\n\nFor the\n\nother\n\nbands,\n\nthe\n\ndifferences\n\nin the adjustments\nare less.\nA 1% factor is significant,\nif the calibrations\nof the SeaWiFS\nbands are to be made\nconsistent\nat the 2% level.\n\n2.8\n\nCONCLUDING\n\nREMARKS\n\nFor SeaWiFS,\nthe conversion\nof the spectral\nshape dependence\nin the SeaWiFS laboratory\ncalibration\nto orbit is\ndone in two steps. For air masses of five or less, the fractional\ndecrease\nin the response\nof band 8 is 0.1% or less.\nFor\nthe other SeaWiFS\nbands, tile effects of atmospheric\noxygen A-band\nabsorption\nare much smaller.\nAtmospheric\nabsorption\nand emission features do not expand the residual uncertainties\nill the corrections\npresented\nhere. They\nremain at about 0.2%, both for total and out-of-band\nrespouse.\nStudies of atmospheric\neffects oil ocean color imagery,\ne.g., Ding and Gordon (1995), used detailed\nmodels of tile\natmospheric\nand oceanic contributors\nto the top-of-theatmosphere\nradiances,\nbut used a simplified\nmodel of the\nresponse\nof the instrument.\nFor Ding and Gordon (1995),\nSeaWiFS\nband 7 was assumed\nto have a square wave response with wavelength,\nwith the band response outside of\nthe band edges (the 50% response points) set to zero and\nthe response\ninside the band edges set to a constant\nvalue.\nIt is now possible to combine\nthe actual response\nof\nthe SeaWiFS\ninstrument\nwith detailed\nmodels of the atmosphere\nand ocean, since ocean color imagery from SeaWiFS is an amalgam\nof the top-of-the-atmosphere\nradiances and the instrument.\nIn the concept\nof Evans and\nGordon\n(1994),\nhowever,\nthe imagery\nderives from the\nocean atmosphere\ninstrument\nsystem.\n\nR.A.Barnes,\nE-n.Yeh,andR.E.Eplee\n\nChapter\nA Comparison\nand\n\nthe\n\n3\n\nof the Spectral\nResponses\nof\nSeaWiFS\nTransfer\nRadiometer\n\nSeaWiFS\n\nROBERT A. BARNES\nGeneral Sciences\nCorporation\nLaurel, Maryland\nABSTRACT\nBoth SeaWiFS\nand the SXR are filter radiometers\nwith finite bandwidths.\nThe use of these instruments\nrequires\na knowledge of the effects that finite bandwidths\ncause. These effects also depend on the source\'s spectral\nshape,\nwhich the instruments\nmeasure.\nFor the radiometric\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe source is the GSFC SIS. The\nBSRs and the ECWs\nbe needed to convert\n\n3.1\n\nfor SeaWiFS and the SXR,\nthe SXR measured\nspectral\n\nINSTRUMENT\n\nwhen measuring\nthe GSFC SIS, are presented\nradiances\nto the SeaWiFS\nwavelengths.\n\nMODELS\n\nSix of the SeaWiFS\nbands have bandwidths\n(full-width\nat half-maximum)\nof 20nm;\nthe other two have bandwidths of 40 nm (Barnes\net al. 1994a). These bandwidths\nare sufficiently\nwide to cause the output from SeaWiFS\nto\nbe dependent\non the spectral\nshape of the source it measures.\nOn orbit, the measurements\nfrom each SeaWiFS\nband involve a range of wavelengths:\nLe =\n\n/0\n\nLe()_)R()_)d)_,\n\n(16)\n\nwhere Le (A) is the Earth-exiting\nspectral radiance at wavelength A, and R(_) is the spectral\nresponse of the band at\nwavelength\n,k. By this definition,\nL e is the Earth-exiting\nspectral\nradiance integrated\nover the SeaWiFS spectral response.\nFor the SeaWiFS Project,\nspectral radiance is expressed\nin mWcm-2sr\n-1 #m -1. For SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\non\norbit, the spectral\nshape of the Earth-exiting\nradiance\nis\nnever known exactly.\nThe calibration\nof the instrument\nis forced to use approximations\nto that on-orbit\nspectral\nradiance.\nA study of several spectral\napproximations\ncan\nbe found in Barnes and Yeh (1996).\nThe output\nfrom each SeaWiFS\nband is the solution\nof the integral in (16), plus the inclusion of several multiplicative\nconstants\nthat have no spectral\ndependence.\nFor\ntile SeaWiFS\nProject,\nradiance,\ni.e., the solution\nto (16),\nis expressed\nin mWcm -2 sr -1. For most uses of the SeaWiFS measurements,\nthe radiance\nfor each band must be\nexpressed\nin a form that represents\nthe spectral\nradiance\nfrom the source at a given wavelength.\nThis representation\nwill be discussed\nbelow.\n\nhere.\n\nThey\n\nwill\n\nThe spectral\nresponse of each band is given in terms of\nmilliamperes\nof current from the photodiode\nin the detector per unit of spectral radiance.\nThe photodiode\nperforms\nthe integration\nfor the instrument.\nIt converts the integral\nin (15) into an electric current.\nThe electronics\nwithin the\ninstrument\nconvert\nthis current\ninto volts and then into\ndigital counts.\nThese conversions\nare multiplicative\nwith\nno spectral\ndependence.\nThere are practical\nlimits to the wavelength\nranges of\nthe spectral\nresponses\nof the SeaWiFS\nbands.\nOutside\nof\nthose wavelength\nlimits, the responses\nof the bands are\nnext to zero. For example,\nthere is a limited\nwavelength\nrange over which the silicon photodiodes\nrespond\nto incoming flux (see Fig. 11 of Barnes et al. 1994b).\nIn the\nlaboratory\ncharacterization\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe wavelength\ninterval over which the band spectral\nresponses\nhave been\ndetermined\nextends\nfrom 380-1,150nm.\nFor most of this\ninterval,\nthe responses\nof the individual\nbands are effectively zero (Barnes et al. 1994b).\nFor the laboratory\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe spectral\nshape of the radiance from the source, i.e., from the GSFC\nSIS, is known (Barnes 1996a). In the laboratory,\ntherefore,\nthe measurement\nof the sphere radiance\nby a SeaWiFS\nband can be expressed\nas\n\nLs =\n\n1150\n\nLs(_)R()_)d)_,\n\n(17)\n\nd 380\n\nwhere Ls(A) is the spectral\nradiance\nfrom the integrating\nsphere at wavelength\nA. calibration\nof\nSeaWiFS.\n\nR.A.Barnes,\nE-n.Yeh,andR.E.Eplee\n\nChapter\n\n4\n\nCenter\n\nWavelengths\n\nSeaWiFS\n\nROBERT A.\nGeneral\n\nSciences\n\nBARNES\n\nCorporation,\n\nLaurel,\n\nMaryland\n\nABSTRACT\n\nWith its 20 and 40 nm bandwidths,\nSeaWiFS\ndoes not make measurements\nat individual\nwavelengths.\nRather,\nthe output\nfrom each SeaWiFS\nband can be considered\nas an integral\nover a finite wavelength\nregion of the\nsource it measures.\nTo make SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\nuseful, it is important\nto relate the measured\nradiance\nfrom the instrument\nto a reference wavelength.\nThat wavelength\nconnects\neach SeaWiFS\nmeasurement\nto the\nspectral\nradiance\nfrom the source at that wavelength.\nFor the initial calibration\nof SeaWiFS,\nthe reference center\nwavelengths\nwere those from the instrument\'s\nperformance\nspecifications.\nA set of BCWs for on-orbit\nuse are\npresented\nhere. The use of these new wavelengths\nwill require a revision of the on-orbit\nradiometric\ncalibration\nconstants\nfor the instrument.\n\n4.1\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nFor the development\nof the initial SeaWiFS\ncalibration equations\n(Barnes et al. 1994b), the instrument\nwas\nassumed\nto have a set of eight fixed center wavelengths,\none for each band.\nFor SeaWiFS\nbands 1-8, these wavelengths are 412, 443, 490, 510, 555, 670, 765, and 865 nm,\nrespectively.\nThese wavelengths\ncome from the instrument\nspecifications\n(Barnes et al. 1994a), and they are the wavelengths at which the laboratory\nsource for the SeaWiFS\nradiometric\ncalibration\nwas itself calibrated.\nThese center\nwavelengths\nare embedded\nin the radiometric\ncalibration\nof SeaV\xc2\xa2iFS. For the current\ncalibration\nof the instrument,\nthey are the de facto center wavelengths\nfor the instrument. Since the development\nof the original SeaWiFS\ncalibration\nequations,\nstudies were performed\nto investigate\nthe effects of different source spectral shapes on the output\nof the SeaWiFS\nbands (Barnes 1996a, and Barnes and Yeh\n1996). In addition,\nthese studies used the de facto center\nwavelengths\nfor each band and calculated\nchanges in the\noutput from the bands for different source spectral\nshapes\nnormalized\nat those wavelengths.\nThose studies were particularly\ninstructive\nin developing\nan approach\nfor calculating the out-of-band\nresponses\nof the SeaWiFS bands for\ndifferent source spectral\nshapes.\nIt is also possible,\nhowever,\nto examine the effects of\nsource spectral\nshape in terms of fixed radiometric\noutput\nfrom the SeaWiFS bands, accompanied\nby changes in their\ncenter wavelengths\nwith changes in source spectral\nshape.\nThe groundwork\nfor this approach\nwas presented\nin a comparison of the spectral\nresponses\nof SeaWiFS\nand the SeaWiFS Transfer\nRadiometer\n(SXR) (Barnes\n1996c). That\n\ncomparison\ninstruments\nthe source\n\ncentered on the in-band\nresponses\nof the two\nfor the spectral\nshape of tile GSFC sphere_\nto be used in the recalibration\nof SeaWiFS.\n\nIn spectral\nterms,\nthe output\nfrom the GSFC sphere\nvaries smoothly\nwith wavelength\nover tile extended\nbandpasses of the SeaWiFS\nbands (Barnes\n1996a).\nThis allows the calculation\nof the wavelength\nat which the spectral radiance\nfrom the sphere equals the BSR from each\ninstrument\nband.\nThe same smooth\nchanges\nwith wavelength are found in the Planck function\ncurves that were\nused as approximations\nto the spectral shapes of the Earthexiting spectral\nradiance\nwhich SeaWiFS\nwill view on orbit (Barnes\n1996a and Barnes and Yeh 1996). Eventually,\nthese approximations\nto the Earth-exiting\nspectral\nradiances may be replaced with spectral\nshapes based on atmospheric\nradiometric\ntransfer\nmodels.\nThe adoption\nof\nthese more realistic\nspectral\nshapes will require a modification of the results presented\nhere.\n\n4.2\n\nINSTRUMENT\n\nRESPONSE\n\nAs part of a comparison\nof tile spectral\nresponses\nof\nSeaWiFS\nand the SXR, Barnes\n(1996c) examined\nthe effects of tile finite bandwidths\nof the two instruments.\nTile\ndiscussion\nin this section has been extracted\nfrom that report.\nFor a SeaWiFS\nband, it is possible\nBSR, using the band\'s spectral\nresponse\nfunction:\nLB(AB)\n\n=\n\nf;?AL_(A)R(A)dA\n\nto calculate\nthe\nas the weighting\n\n(2i)\n\nf:(? The\nSeaWiFS\ninstrument\nmeasures\nthe integral of spectral\nradiance (Barnes\n1996c).\nThis integral\nis the numerator\nof\n(21).\nAgain,\nfor the discussions\nhere, the integral\nthat\nSeaWiFS\nmeasures\nis called "radiance"\nand does not have\nthe term per micron\n(#m-l).\nTo make SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\nuseful, it is important\nto relate the measured\nradiance\nfrom the instrument\nto a reference\nwavelength.\nThat wavelength\nconnects\nthe SeaWiFS\nmeasurement\nto\nthe spectral\nradiance\nfrom the source at that wavelength.\nFor the vast majority\nof discussions\nof SeaWiFS\nmeasurements in the literature,\nthe terms radiance\nand spectral\nradiance\nare used interchangeably.\nIn those presentations,\nradiance\nmeans the same as spectral\nradiance\nhere.\nIn a similar\nmanner,\nthe BCW associated\nwith this\nspectral\nradiance\ncan be calculated,\nusing Ls(A)R(A)\nas\nthe weighting\n\nfunction.\n\nAB =\n\nf:_ALs(A)R(A)dA\nf_\\_Ls(A)R(A)dA\n\nwhere AB is the BCW.\nAlternately,\nit is possible\n\n(22)\n\'\n\nPlanck function curves normalized\nat 412 nm to the typical\nspectral\nradiance for SeaWiFS\nband 1. These three curves\nprovide approximations\nto the Earth-exiting\nspectral\nradiances that SeaWiFS\nwill view on orbit. Of the three, the\n12,000K\nPlanck function\nis considered\nto have the best\noverall fit to the typical\nspectral\nradiances.\nThe BCWs\nand ECWs are calculated\nbelow using these source spectral\nshapes. Because the calculation\nof the center wavelengths\ndepends solely on the spectral\nshapes of the source functions and those of the band responses,\nthe absolute\nvalues\nof the source functions\nare unimportant.\nFor the calculations presented\nhere, the Planck functions\nhave been normalized to unity at 380 nm.\nThe center wavelengths\nare also calculated\nbelow using\nthe spectral\nshape of the output\nof the GSFC sphere.\nIn\nBarnes and Yeh (1996), each lamp setting for the sphere\nwas considered\nas having an independent\nspectral\nshape.\nIn that study, the minor differences\nbetween\nlamp settings\nwere important\nbecause\nthe results\nwere a fundamental\npart of the recalibration\nof SeaWiFS.\nFor the purposes\nof\nthis report, the spectral\nshapes from the four lamp settings\nhave been combined\ninto one representative\nshape for the\nsphere, since for this report, the sphere\'s\nspectral\nshape is\nused only for comparisons\nwith the Planck functions.\nFinally, a constant\nsource function\nof unity is used below for calculations\nof BCWs for each band. (1994b).\nThe\nsystem level center wavelengths,\nin turn, were calculated\nas the center point of the full-width\nat half-maximum\nfor\neach band. It is\npossible, however, to derive a revised on-orbit\ncalibration\nof SeaWiFS\nat the BCWs, using the spectral\ncomparison\nof SeaWiFS\nand the SXR (Barnes\n1996c), along with the\nout-of-band\nresults from Barnes and Yeh (1996).\nIt is also possible\nto convert\nthe SeaWiFS\nmeasured\nspectral\nradiances\nto wavelengths\nother than those in this\nstudy.\nThis is important,\nbecause\nSeaWiFS\'s\nmeasurements will be compared\nwith those from other satellite\ninstruments;\nthese other instruments\nwill measure\nat wavelengths different from, but close to, SeaWiFS.\nOCTS, on\nboard the Advanced\nEarth Observation\nSatellite (ADEOS1) was launched\nin August 1996, and MODIS on board the\nEarth Observing\nSystem\'s morning platform\n(EOS-AM)\nis\nscheduled\nfor launch in 1998.\nBoth of these instruments\nhave ocean color bands at wavelengths\nsimilar to SeaWiFS.\nIf the measurements\nof Earth-exiting\nspectral\nradiances\nfrom each of these instruments\nare to be compared\nfor uniformity, then the wavelengths\nat which the measurements\nare made must be clearly determined.\nFor SeaWiFS,\nit will\nbe possible to convert the measured\nspectral\nradiances\non\norbit to the spectral\nradiances\nat the wavelengths\nof the\nother instruments.\nFinally, it is possible to provide a better source spectral shape than a 12,000 K Planck function\nfor corrections\nto SeaWiFS\nmeasurements\non orbit. This can be done by\nmeans of an atmospheric\nradiative\ntransfer\nmodel, which is\noutside the scope of this study. Alternately,\nit will be possible to use initial flight data from the instrument\nto provide\nan updated\nset of typical spectral\nradiances\nas the basis for\na better model of the source spectral\nshape, which will be\nviewed during SeaWiFS\nocean color diffuser\n\nsolar-diffuser\nchanges\n\nlunar\n\nof the\n\nmeasurements\n\nis possible\nchange\n\nand\n\nseparate\n\nusing\n\nrettectance\n\nof diffuser\n\nmeasurements,\nshort-term\n\nsimultaneous\nit\n\nin\n\ndiffuser.\n\nWith\n\nnormalized\nuse\n\nthe\n\nthe\n\nmeasurements,\n\nby\n\nmssumt)t.ion\n\nreflectivity\n\nto\n\nthe\nhmar\nof\n\nidentif)\'\n\na\n\nR.A. Barnes,\n\nE-n. Yeh, and R.E. Eplee\n\nstep changes in instrument\nsensitivity\nbetween lunar measurements.\nSuch step changes were found in CZCS measurements\n(Evans and Gordon\n1994).\nDetermining\nthese changes waits for the launch of SeaWiFS and the initiation\nof on-orbit operations.\nThis chapter reports on the design of the diffuser and its prelaunch\ncharacterization\nand calibration.\n\n5.2\n\nDIFFUSER\n\nDESCRIPTION\n\nOverviews\nof the operation\nof the SeaWiFS\nscanner\nwere presented\nin Barnes and Holmes (1993) and in Barnes\net al. (1994b).\nThis section concentrates\non a description\nof the solar diffuser, itself. Figure 26 shows the SeaWiFS\ninstrument,\nthe coordinate\nsystem for the instrument,\nand\nthe spacecraft.\nThe flat plate at the top of the instrument\nis mounted\nto the SeaStar\nspacecraft\n(not shown),\nand\nnadir is the +x direction.\nUsing the coordinate\nsystem in\nFig. 26, SeaWiFS and SeaStar both fly in the -y direction,\nand SeaWiFS\nscans west-to-east\nin the xz plane, or normal\nto the instrument\'s\ny axis. The solar diffuser (also called\nthe solar calibrator)\npoints in the +y direction,\nthat is,\nto the aft of the spacecraft.\nOn the sunlit side of the\nEarth,\nthe spacecraft\nmoves from north to south; it has\na descending\nequatorial\ncrossing.\nAs a result, the diffuser\nviews tim sun as the spacecraft\npasses over the Earth\'s\nSouth Pole.\nFigure 27 is a drawing of the diffuser assembly mounted\nto the sensor.\nWith the sun to the right of the drawing,\nsunlight\npasses through\nthe attenuator,\nis scattered\noff the\ndiffuser, and enters tile instrument\nto the left. The diffuser\nplate itself is part of the diffuser housing.\nIt is painted on\nthe inside surface of the housing, behind the diffuser cover\nin Fig. 27. The figure shows the back of the diffuser cover;\nthe back of the diffuser cover is also painted\nand acts as a\nsecond diffuser.\nThe cover has a spring-loaded\nhinge at its\nbottom\nand is held in the vertical\nposition\nby a solenoid\nactuator.\nWhen the one-time\nactuator\nreleases the cover,\nthe diffuser cover rotates\ninto the page and comes to rest\noil the bottom of the diffuser housing, held in place by the\nspring on the hinge. A schematic\nof the diffuser assembly,\nas seen from above the instrument,\nis shown in Fig. 28.\nThe figure shows the assembly\nwithout the diffuser cover.\nThe diffuser\nhas a nominal\nillumination\nangle of 60 \xc2\xb0\nto avoid possible specular\nglints from its surface and to\nreduce the flux density on the diffuser plate by a factor of\ntwo. The plate and diffuser covers have coatings\nof YBT1\npaint. This paint provides a durable flat white coating with\nproven orbital stability,\nas shown in the results from the\nLong Duration\nExposure\nFacility (LDEF) flown by NASA.\nThe wavelength\ndependence\nof the reflectivity\nof a YB71\ncoated plate, as measured\nat SBRC, is shown in Fig. 29.\nThe attenuator\nis a flat black anodized\naluminum\naperture plate with holes which reduce the solar flux on the\ndiffuser (Fig. 30). The small hole size (4.32 mm diameter)\nand the close spacing of the holes (6.35 mm centers) in the\n\nattenuator\nensures that images of the sun partially\noverlap\non the diffuser, providing\na uniformly\nilluminated\nsurface.\nThe aperture\nholes were drilled in a pattern\nthat was rotated 35 \xc2\xb0 to prevent\nstriping of the diffuser by photolyzed\norganic\ncompounds\nover the 5-year life of the SeaWiFS\nmission.\nThe spacing between the diffuser and the attenuator is such that reflections\nfrom the back of the attenuator\nonto the diffuser account for less than 1% of the total illumination.\nThe attenuator\nhas a uniform thickness\nof 2.54 mm and\nis tilted by 30 \xc2\xb0 about\nthe x axis.\nThis design prevents\nattenuator\nglints from reaching the diffuser.\nIt also reduces\nillumination\nvariations\nas the incidence\nangle of the sun\nvaries over the seasons.\nOver each SeaWiFS\norbit, the\ninstrument\nrotates\n(pitches)\nabout\nthe z axis (Fig. 26).\nHowever, because of the inclination\nof the Earth\'s\naxis to\nthe plane of the Earth\'s\norbit and the inclination\nof the\nsatellite\'s\norbit about the Earth,\nthere is also a relative\nmotion\nof the solar incidence\nangle with respect\nto the\ninstrument\'s\nxz plane over each year. This motion\nhas a\nrange of +5 \xc2\xb0 about the x axis. It is the effect of this angular\nchange that the tilted attenuator\nreduces (Fig. 31).\n\n5.3\n\nLAB\n\nMEASUREMENTS\n\nThe SeaWiFS\ndiffuser acts to convert\nthe input irradiance from the sun into an output\nradiance\nto be measured by the instrument.\nThe input irradiance\nhas units\nof Wcm-2pm\n-1 and the output\nradiance\nhas units of\nWcm-2#m-lsr-1,\nwhere sr is the SI unit symbol\nfor\nsteradian\n(which is a unit of solid angle).\nA radiance\nmeasurement\nis made of a source that is large enough\nthat\nthe solid angle of its surface is greater than the solid angle\nviewed by the instrument.\nFor example,\nSeaV_riFS has been\ndesigned\nso that each radiance\nmeasurement\nhas a solid\nangle equivalent\nto an area of 1.1xl.1 km at the Earth\'s\nsurface at nadir. For SeaWiFS,\nthe diffuser is measured\nas\na radiance source, but the sun is an irradiance\nsource.\nThe\nsolar flux is not parsed into individual\nunits of solid angle,\nbut is used as the flux from the entire surface area of the\nsun.\n\nThe ratio of the output radiance to the input irradiance\nis known as the BRDF, with units of inverse steradians.\nA\nperfect,\n100% reflective\nLambertian\ndiffuser has a BRDF\nof 7r-_, or 0.318. For SeaWiFS,\nthe instrument\ngains were\nset to measure the ocean, which is a relatively\ndark light\nsource, compared\nwith the sun. As a result, the SeaWiFS\ndiffuser required\na smaller BRDF,\ncorresponding\nto 10%\nof a perfect Lambertian\ndiffuser.\nThis requirement\ndetermined the diffuser design, with the diffuser plate tilted at\nan angle of 60 \xc2\xb0 to the solar illumination\nand with the attenuator\ncontaining\nan array of small holes.\n\n5.3.1\n\nReflectance\n\nat\n\nNormal\n\nIncidence\n\nIn order to properly test the SeaWiFS\ndiffuser, it was illuminated\nwith a source having an angular subtense\nsimilar\n\n55\n\nSeaWiFS alibration\nC\nTopics,\nPart1\nSOLAR\n\nCALIBRATOR\n\n\'_+Z\n+X\nNORTH\n\nFig. 26. Forthe calcuThe concept\nfor the experiment\nis simple.\nThe simulations,he reflectance\nt\nofthe pressedalondiffuser late\nh\np\nlation requires\nonly the direct solar beam, and the beam\nwas0.99/_r normalncidence.\nat\ni\n5.3.2\n\nBRDF\n\nNormal incidence\nfor the SeaWiFS\ndiffuser lies along\nthe +y vector,\nthat is, normal to the xz plane, facing aft\n(see Fig. 26). On each orbit, SeaWiFS\nrotates once about\nits z axis. For the BRDF measurements\nof the SeaWiFS\ndiffuser\nby the manufacturer,\nthis rotation\nwas called "a\nchange in elevation\nangle."\nOver the course of a year, the\ndirection\nof the sun, relative\nto the xz plane, changes\nas\na rotation\nabout the x axis. For the SeaWiFS calibration,\nthis rotation\nwas called "a change in azimuth\nangle."\nThe BRDF for the SeaWiFS\ndiffuser was measured\nfor\nelevation\nand azimuth\nangles from the normal\nincidence\nvector\n(+y axis).\nThese values are shown for SeaWiFS\nbands 4 (Table 28) and 8 (Table 29). The values are normalized to 100% at zero azimuth\nand zero elevation.\nThose\n100% values\n\ncan be converted\n\nto reflectances\n\nusing the cor-\n\nmust illuminate\nthe diffuser at the same incidence\nangle\nused during on-orbit\nmeasurements.\nAtmospheric\neffects\non the direct beam in the ground\nmeasurements\nmust be\ndetermined,\nand corrections\nfor these effects must be applied to the ground results.\nA discussion\nof this correction\nprocedure\nis given by Biggar et al. (1994).\nThe experiment\nafter corrections\nfor stray light in the\ninstrument\n(Biggar et al. 1995) provided a set of predicted\ntop-of-the-atmosphere\ndigital counts from SeaWiFS.\nThese\nvalues will be compared\nwith digital counts from on-orbit\ndiffuser measurements\nduring\nthe initial check of the instrument.\nIn addition,\nthe experiment\nafter corrections\nfor stray light in the instrument\nprovided\na comparison\nbetween\nthe outdoor\n(solar radiation-based)\nand the indoor (integrating\nsphere-based)\ndeterminations\nof the radiometric\nsensitivity\nof SeaWiFS.\nIn this comparison,\nthe\ndifferences\nbetween the two methods were less than 3% for\nall eight SeaWiFS
